<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_137313685">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137313685" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4057501-karissa" class="left imgcol" title="Karissa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1415884708p2/4057501.jpg" alt="Karissa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/137313685?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 30, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4057501-karissa" class="user" title="Karissa">Karissa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer137313685">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16184537650129254268">This is the first book in Alexander's Westmark Trilogy. I read Alexander's "Chronicles of Prydain" when I was in middle school and really liked it. When I realized that I had never read this series I had to grab it up. It is a wonderful young adult series and the fact that it still reads well (over twenty years after being written) is a testament to Alexander's exceptional style of fantasy writing.<br /><br />Theo is an apprentice to a printer in a time where the monarchy is becoming increasingly stringent</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16184537650129254268">This is the first book in Alexander's Westmark Trilogy. I read Alexander's "Chronicles of Prydain" when I was in middle school and really liked it. When I realized that I had never read this series I had to grab it up. It is a wonderful young adult series and the fact that it still reads well (over twenty years after being written) is a testament to Alexander's exceptional style of fantasy writing.<br /><br />Theo is an apprentice to a printer in a time where the monarchy is becoming increasingly stringent about printed materials. During an inspection Theo and his master run afoul of the Royal inspectors and in a fit of rage Theo smashes one of the inspectors with a part of the printing press. Theo's master is killed and Theo becomes a criminal on the run. Shortly after fleeing town he meet up with Dr. Absalom, who is not as much of a doctor as he says and much more a rogue, and a midget named Musket. There crazy adventures eventually have them grouping up with Mickle, a girl thief who is much more than she first seems. Theo partakes in some wild adventures and struggles with the morals of the livelihood he is making as a rogue, he eventually tries to make a honest living, but that only ends up with him deeply embroiled in politics that could make or break the country.<br /><br />This book was a wonderful classic young adult fantasy. You have Cabbarus, the evil chief minister, who is a wonderful villain. You have an ailing king, who puts the lineage of the throne at risk. Theo is a young boy who struggles with his solid morals, as actions that should be honorable end up not being right. The characters are well-woven and make for interesting reading. In fact the characters show an admirable level of maturity and reasoning throughout the book. The plot moves a little slow, but everything that happens in this book happens for a reason so it is a very tightly woven story. The writing is excellent and fits the mode of the story perfectly.<br /><br />There are some excellent actions scenes, lots of intrigue, and plots woven within plots. This is a book that makes you think in order to follow all the threads of plot that are happening. There is a lot of delving into morals and violence and what is wrong and right. I was amazed at how complex the story was for such a short book. Definitely an excellent read. It would be appropriate for all ages, but younger readers might not be able to follow all the intrigue and ethics discussion that goes on.<br /><br />I look forward to reading the next book in this series The Kestrel. "Westmark" is a must read for young adult fantasy lovers. The plot may drag a bit at parts, but the story is intricate and interesting. I did like "The Chronicles of Prydain" better; those books were an easier and more engaging read, but "Westmark" is close in quality and definitely a classic. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16184537650129254268">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_243378899">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/243378899" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1067441-sara" class="left imgcol" title="Sara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1207797858p2/1067441.jpg" alt="Sara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/243378899?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 14, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1067441-sara" class="user" title="Sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1067441-sara?shelf=beloved-childhood-books" class="actionLinkLite">beloved-childhood-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer243378899">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5201809917401398063">Lloyd Alexander remains one of my enduring beloved authors. Having read and loved the Prydain Chronicles as an elementary-school student (and to this day, frankly), I discovered his Westmark Trilogy in (I think) either late middle school or early high school, and was utterly enchanted. It was also a revelation to me: fantasy didn't have to be medieval!!! It didn't even have to have nonhuman characters or any magical trappings at all. Sharing the adventures of Theo and friends in the pseudo-18th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5201809917401398063">Lloyd Alexander remains one of my enduring beloved authors. Having read and loved the Prydain Chronicles as an elementary-school student (and to this day, frankly), I discovered his Westmark Trilogy in (I think) either late middle school or early high school, and was utterly enchanted. It was also a revelation to me: fantasy didn't have to be medieval!!! It didn't even have to have nonhuman characters or any magical trappings at all. Sharing the adventures of Theo and friends in the pseudo-18th century setting of Westmark is still something I revisit every other year or so, and they've never lost their delight for me. That being said, however, the Westmark trilogy (particularly after this first book), definitely qualifies as one of Lloyd Alexander's 'darker' writings. (Mind you, what passes for dark for Mr. Alexander still isn't hugely dark, and that's quite all right.) There is murder, and betrayal, and--as Theo learns--black-and-white morality is a difficult and extremely rare beast. But there is also love and friendship and learning that some things are worth fighting and dying for.<br /><br />As is to be expected from an Alexander book, the cast of characters is colorful, the villain is appropriately horrible (really horrible, in fact), and (as is common with Alexander heroes) Theo-the-protagonist spends a great deal of time charmingly bewildered and outright frustrated. He has a harder time of it than, say, Taran of Caer Dalben. Although the two characters share a certain commonality--both do their growing up in a country torn by war--Theo is a young man of very black-and-white morality and a certian rigid honesty who is very much an everyman: what he'd really like is to be left alone to do an honest job for an honest wage. Instead, his master, who runs a printing press, is murdered by government soldiers and Theo--having struck out at one of the men--finds himself on the run for (he believes) murder. In order to survive, he finds himself taking up with thieves and conmen and discovers--to his horror--that he has a certain flair for con-artistry. Unwilling to give up his views of the world as black and white, he runs away a second time (despite falling in love with the street urchin Mickle) and tries once again to make what he considers an honest living as a letter-writer (being able to read and write well, he offers his services to those who cannot), and before long ends up to his eyeballs in revolutionaries. He agrees with their cause--enough to actually join them--but struggles with the dubious morality many of them embrace in regards to things he finds distasteful. Eventually, he is reunited with Mickle, who is not quite what she seems, and rather than finding his life settled into a neat, orderly compartment as he dearly wished Theo finds himself in an even bigger tangle, thus setting things up for the next two books.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5201809917401398063">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_438144310">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/438144310" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9059322-vaughn-hokanson" class="left imgcol" title="Vaughn Hokanson"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1347906101p2/9059322.jpg" alt="Vaughn Hokanson" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/438144310?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9059322-vaughn-hokanson" class="user" title="Vaughn Hokanson">Vaughn Hokanson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9059322-vaughn-hokanson?shelf=1-12" class="actionLinkLite">1-12</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer438144310">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14819005898700925945">This is the story of a young apprentice printer in the fictional kingdom of Westmark; Westmark feels a little like pre-industrial America, and the story revolves around tension between freedom fighting revolutionaries and a corrupt royal minister and inept monarchy. Theo gets caught up in all of this when his print master Anton is killed by the thugs working for the evil chief minister Carrabas who has essentially taken over rule of the kingdom as the King grieves for his missing and presumed to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14819005898700925945">This is the story of a young apprentice printer in the fictional kingdom of Westmark; Westmark feels a little like pre-industrial America, and the story revolves around tension between freedom fighting revolutionaries and a corrupt royal minister and inept monarchy. Theo gets caught up in all of this when his print master Anton is killed by the thugs working for the evil chief minister Carrabas who has essentially taken over rule of the kingdom as the King grieves for his missing and presumed to be dead daughter, princess Augusta. Anton is killed in order to stop the printing of documents that Carrabas deems detrimental to the crown, so Theo finds himself on his own. He takes up with a traveling salesman/magician/fraud named Las Bambas who travels from town to town with his assistant Muskit the dwarf trying to concoct any hokum scam he can to make money. Soon a street urchin named Mickle who adds her unique talents to this company of misfits joins in their adventures and scams. The group eventually gets mixed up with a group of anti monarchy rebels and also find out that they are more linked to the crown than they could have imagined.<br /><br />One of the things that I liked most about this story is that it poses a lot of interesting questions about politics, power, freedom of speech, and the rule of law without ruining the story. Occasionally the plot felt quite predictable and a little contrived, but the internal dilemmas of the characters made the plot less important and encouraged me to do some good thinking about my own perspectives on the importance and influence of government and the forces government to change. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14819005898700925945">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_446929700">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/446929700" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/398239-michelle" class="left imgcol" title="Michelle"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448427826p2/398239.jpg" alt="Michelle" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/446929700?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 02, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/398239-michelle" class="user" title="Michelle">Michelle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/398239-michelle?shelf=la-la-loved" class="actionLinkLite">la-la-loved</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/398239-michelle?shelf=ya" class="actionLinkLite">ya</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/398239-michelle?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/398239-michelle?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/398239-michelle?shelf=2012" class="actionLinkLite">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer446929700">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2026013745784160158">On the run and facing imprisonment (or worse) Theo runs head-long into the finest charlatan ever to walk the streets of Westmark, Count Las Bombas and his dwarf companion, Musket. Although uncomfortable with their easy style of thievery, Theo is reluctant to part company after they are joined by the street urchin Mickle and eventually travel to the palace itself to face the Chief Minster Carrabas, who is determined to take control of Westmark for himself.<br /><br />Fast-paced yet utterly subtle in it's det</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2026013745784160158">On the run and facing imprisonment (or worse) Theo runs head-long into the finest charlatan ever to walk the streets of Westmark, Count Las Bombas and his dwarf companion, Musket. Although uncomfortable with their easy style of thievery, Theo is reluctant to part company after they are joined by the street urchin Mickle and eventually travel to the palace itself to face the Chief Minster Carrabas, who is determined to take control of Westmark for himself.<br /><br />Fast-paced yet utterly subtle in it's detail Westmark was a revelation to me. I don't think I have ever read anything so tightly written with such deliberate adventure but still chock-full of complex discussions on right and wrong. By allowing the reader to follow Theo on his travels through Westmark, we are able to witness as he evolves and even get frustrated as his ideas of right and wrong are challenged by every single character he meets along the way. Yet due to stellar pacing and superb writing the book is never bogged down into a preachy mess.<br /><br />Weighing in at just under 200 pages, Westmark packs quite a punch. Lloyd Alexander caught me over and over again with his seemingly innocent yet startling phrases like this opening line which reads:<br /><br />Theo, by occupation, was a devil.<br /><br />Which although being perfectly true, doesn't exactly mean what you think it might. You see, Theo is a printer's apprentice and those apprentices go by the title of a printer's devil. Makes perfect sense, no? But at the same time extremely memorable and a technique he uses to perfection throughout the entire series. I never got tired of these little flashes of creative genius. They never failed to catch my full attention with very little effort and usually with stunning results. Matched with the cleverness of how Alexander would then bring each detail to light was absolutely lovely. If this is what every Lloyd Alexander book reads like, you can be sure I'll be blazing through his oeuvre without delay.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2026013745784160158">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12069934480602131674"> <em>Westmark</em> starts with one of the best lines I’ve read in quite a while: “Theo, by occupation, was a devil.” On the next page, concluding the description of our hero, are the two sentences, “As for Theo, he loved virtue, despised injustice, and was always slightly hungry. Apart from that, he was reasonably happy.”<br /> Theo’s reasonably happy existence is about to end, however. Westmark’s king, in mourning for his lost daughter, has allowed his power-hungry chief minister, Cabbarus, to impose all sort</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12069934480602131674">	<em>Westmark</em> starts with one of the best lines I’ve read in quite a while: “Theo, by occupation, was a devil.” On the next page, concluding the description of our hero, are the two sentences, “As for Theo, he loved virtue, despised injustice, and was always slightly hungry. Apart from that, he was reasonably happy.”<br />	Theo’s reasonably happy existence is about to end, however. Westmark’s king, in mourning for his lost daughter, has allowed his power-hungry chief minister, Cabbarus, to impose all sorts of restrictions on the populace. When Theo’s master and parental substitute is killed while defending his right to free press, Theo goes on the run. He meets up with a quack doctor who fleeces gullible citizens for a living; although Theo finds this work morally repugnant, he also sees a sense of honor in the doctor, especially compared with the despotic rule of Cabbarus. His sense of ethics continues to develop as he meets other people, including an anti-monarchist group, and forms a connection with a young waif with a talent for ventriloquism. Action in the palace and in underground meeting places all comes together in a satisfying finale that still leaves plenty of scope for the two remaining books in the trilogy.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12069934480602131674">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer48658829">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15542843256900564549">I read the Prydain series at least 4 or 5 times as a kid, but have been fearful to reread them because I've found Lloyd kind of simple as an adult. I found this in an old box of my books I cleaned up from my parents' house. I never read the series and am enjoying it so far. It has the old-fashioned, slower exposition of the character and situation, along with a very innocent, pure of heart protagonist. It's kind of fun after some more sarcastic stuff I've read lately. And I love the cover! These</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15542843256900564549">I read the Prydain series at least 4 or 5 times as a kid, but have been fearful to reread them because I've found Lloyd kind of simple as an adult. I found this in an old box of my books I cleaned up from my parents' house. I never read the series and am enjoying it so far. It has the old-fashioned, slower exposition of the character and situation, along with a very innocent, pure of heart protagonist. It's kind of fun after some more sarcastic stuff I've read lately. And I love the cover! These old drawing-type covers are so much more interesting than modern YA covers with a flashy movie-type advertisement.<br /><br />Good story, but definitely old fashioned. The narrative develops and tells more about the person's character than a modern book which will often start with an action sequence and not expound so openly on character traits. A lot of fortunate coincidences, but fun book I ignored as it sat on my bookshelf for years as a kid.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15542843256900564549">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/8924.Lloyd_Alexander">About Lloyd Alexander</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/8924.Lloyd_Alexander"><img alt="8924" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1353430382p2/8924.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer1134600926485248997">Lloyd Chudley Alexander (January 30, 1924 - May 17, 2007) was an influential American author of more than forty books, mostly fantasy novels for children and adolescents, as well as several adult books. His most famous contribution to the field of children's literature is the fantasy series The Chronicles of Prydain. The concluding book of the series, <em>The High King</em>, was awarded the Newbery Medal i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1134600926485248997">Lloyd Chudley Alexander (January 30, 1924 - May 17, 2007) was an influential American author of more than forty books, mostly fantasy novels for children and adolescents, as well as several adult books. His most famous contribution to the field of children's literature is the fantasy series The Chronicles of Prydain. The concluding book of the series, <em>The High King</em>, was awarded the Newbery Medal in 1969. Alexander's other books have also won the National Book Award and the American Book Award. He was also one of the creators of Cricket Magazine.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/lloydalexander" target="_blank">http://us.macmillan.com/author/lloyda...</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1134600926485248997">...more</a>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/8924.Lloyd_Alexander" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Lloyd Alexander...</a>
